Film Supplement

The Cry of (City Lights)

— Jia Yan

The important components of drama and film are (implication). Without implication, a film, even if it has good acting, is just an art flower that blooms in the morning and withers in the evening. A drama with good performance and strong implication has permanence. City Lights has profound implications! Equalization of land ownership.

The city's buildings are made by those who have obligations but no rights. The city's civilization is enjoyed by those who have rights but no obligations. After the city develops, the soaring prices and the high cost of real estate drive the city's builders, the poor workers, out of the city. And those who enjoy the city, the ladies and gentlemen, take everything for themselves. The poor workers are not willing to leave the city where they have contributed their blood and sweat. But how can they, (it is not easy to live in the city!)? That is the cry of the city lights!

If the gentlemen who have more than a dozen small houses, villas, and mansions and are unable to be in several places every night would think of the poor workers who have no place to stand. If the young masters who wear imported wool vests and otter skin coats worth hundreds of dollars and sneeze by the stove would think of the poor bones whose tattered quilts are still in the pawnshop and have not been redeemed. If the rich men who go to banquets every day and suffer from indigestion, stomach problems, and toothaches would think of the hungry ghosts who eat congee and rice. If the big men who have too many concubines and suffer from neurasthenia would think of the poor ghosts who starve their wives to death, then it would be good. Class conflicts can be resolved. The terror of communism can be delayed by at least fifty years. Unfortunately, the rich and powerful gentlemen have not thought of this! Too many people are concerned only with their own well-being. Too few people are concerned with the well-being of the masses. They are too focused on their own interests. They have forgotten that it is the responsibility of every rich and powerful gentleman to improve society, seek happiness for the masses, and alleviate the suffering of the people. And only they have this power! Otherwise, the current situation of unequal wealth and extreme differences in suffering will become more prominent, the conflict between rich and poor will become more intense, and it will inevitably lead to a path of mutual destruction!

Although there are many rich and benevolent philanthropists and great living Buddhas in the city, it always feels like a drop in the bucket and does not help much. Charitable undertakings must use scientific methods to have a widespread effect. Giving money on the street to beggars who make a living by kowtowing only increases their dependence. Providing congee and rice can only save them for a while, not for a lifetime. The most complete, most economical, and most permanent charitable undertaking is to revive factories. Because of the Japanese bombing and the impact of the recession, many factories have closed down. Workers are terribly unemployed. It must be known that if factories are not reopened and rebuilt, the wealthy will only earn less money for one day. But the workers will eat less food for one day. The wealthy are afraid of not making money, so they dare not open factories. Instead, they are willing to deposit interest in foreign banks or engage in real estate, raise land prices, and increase the burden of rent on the poor, implementing the wealth-generating technique of (eating human flesh by exploiting the land)! They all want to make money. Why not engage in enterprises that benefit the people instead of doing wealth management that harms others and benefits themselves? If the wealthy are willing to invest in factories, not only can domestic industry be developed, but the poor can also have the opportunity to support themselves. If a family is well-fed and clothed, they will naturally not do anything wrong. That is truly a good thing that will benefit future generations and accumulate immeasurable merit!

Audience Opinions

Review of (A Spray of Plum Blossoms)

— Wan

(A Spray of Plum Blossoms) has been screened at (Central) before and is now showing at West Sea. Not many seats are taken.

Strictly speaking, there are many absurdities in this film. Let me point them out for the public to comment on!

Bai Lede is a powder-puff general and a despicable villain. Since Hu Lunting looked down on him even at school, how could he associate with him? Moreover, upon arriving at someone's house, he peeped into their private quarters and flirted with the ladies of the household. Hu Lunting knew very well about this kind of despicable behavior. Why, when he was leaving, did he entrust his sister to the care of such a person?

Second, Hu Lizhu looked down on Bai Lede from the start. Later, she discovered Bai Lede's plot to harm her brother. But in the end, Lizhu still married him. I suppose this is also called (a karmic relationship)?

Third, it is too far-fetched for a warlord's daughter to be a bodyguard.

There are many other laughable points, but I cannot list them all one by one. However, the actors are all very accomplished. Except for Wang Cilong's winking, which was slightly over the top, Ruan Lingyu's performance as Lizhu was impeccable from beginning to end. Also, the cinematography is not bad. Several artistic landscapes are very outstanding. Therefore, I give this film the overall rating of (good actors, poor director).

Review of (Thoughtful and Meticulous)

— Mei Cun

When I watch foreign films, I often feel that the plot is not tight enough and I don't understand it. In (Thoughtful and Meticulous), the plot is very coherent. Even without reading the synopsis, one can understand the content. Therefore, the director of the film has avoided the problem of looseness in the arrangement of the plot.

The male lead, James Dunn, plays the young lawyer, Peer. Speaking from his position as the main actor, he can only be described as average and competent, without amazing performances. The female lead, Busi Marulan, plays the lady, Helen. I heard that it was her first time on camera. Her attitude is leisurely and elegant, which is truly rare. Regarding her love for the two children left by her sister, her expression of compassion in the scene where Tomman cries out in shock is just right. The two children, played by Pest De Field and Georgia Anisner, are both very good. Their natural and innocent attitude is very brilliant. It can be said that they are pleasing in every scene and are the most colorful part of the whole play.

The scene where Quilly escapes from the window and gathers hundreds of children to surround Bohr's residence is very cleverly interspersed and is the most exciting part of the whole play. Other things, like the situation in the department store, are simple and divided into scenes to meet the needs and do not feel any trouble, which is also satisfactory.

In terms of the plot, it was originally a romantic film, but there is also a deep portrayal of parents' love for their children. Therefore, it can be said that it is (thoughtful and meticulous) not only in terms of love between men and women, but also in terms of parents' love for their children and children's love for their parents. The title of this film is a wonderful blend of the theme and the plot.

Bernard Shaw's Satire on Hollywood

— Hua Ming

When the Irish satirist Bernard Shaw traveled around the world and passed through the United States, the Academy of Motion Picture Arts and Sciences in Hollywood specially contacted Mr. Shaw by long-distance telephone and asked him to go to Hollywood to shoot one or two short films. Mr. Shaw replied in the phone, "I have declined lectures, let alone shooting films?" Later, RKO, Paramount, and Warner Brothers-First National three companies jointly sent representatives to negotiate with Mr. Shaw in person, asking Mr. Shaw to shoot at least a short segment of a short film. Mr. Shaw replied with a smile, "Shaw is old. If he were young and dashing, he would definitely come to win the name of a handsome man. There is no need for your invitation." The representatives still pleaded earnestly. Mr. Shaw also said, "Shaw loves money so much that he is afraid that if he goes to Hollywood, he will not be willing to leave even if he is driven away." A few words scold the general money-grubber.

Several years ago, Samuel Goldwyn, the owner of MGM, traveled to Europe. When he arrived in England, he asked Bernard Shaw to write a script and stated that he was willing to pay a lot of money. Bernard Shaw replied, "Shaw only knows money and does not know art. Mr. Goldwyn is misled by my false reputation." Goldwyn retreated silently.

Bernard Shaw has always been dissatisfied with the so-called (Dollar Man) of the Americans, and especially hates the Hollywood figures who rely on wealth to bully others. Therefore, although there are many dramas in his writings, he has never sold the filming rights to Hollywood for the sake of money.

Ann Harding Will Leave Screen Life

— Huang Yingdai

In Hollywood, another alarming message has recently been reported, saying that Ann Harding will leave screen life. Her good contract expires in the middle of April this year. Although there is news from RKO that the contract will be renewed for another year starting in April, Ann Harding is wondering if she will be selected to continue, because the company has news of cutting actors. It is said that only those with special success in the past can continue to sign the contract. Unfortunately, after consideration, Ann Harding has not been included among the successful actors.

Film Novel

Red Dust (4)

— Jack

("Is this how you waste a good evening?") She asked him in a sociable manner, but Niis didn't answer, always looking at his map.

("Is there anything to eat? I'm starving!") the woman complained.

("The kitchen's in the back," he said with a nod.

("When I was in Saigon, they all said I lost weight. Do you think I lost weight?") She stood in front of him after leaving the table. ("I don't care about that," Niis replied flatly. "Please leave!")

("Hey! Yo!") Yeadan walked to the kitchen. ("There's no need to make such a fuss! I can't go home after a few days. If I had to stay for a month, someone would get drunk and cause you to lose sleep. When you entertain the gorillas in the living room, it's okay for them to make a fuss to you.") She said to herself, looking around the kitchen, she saw a box of biscuits. She reached out to pick a cockroach from the top of the box. ("Ha!") she said in jest. ("Why aren't you in my room?")

She went back to the living room with a box of biscuits. But it was empty. A room door was open along the balcony, giving off light. Niis was undressing. Yeadan took out a biscuit and chewed. She looked around, her eyes betraying a vaguely lost look. In the corner, she saw a parrot in a cage.

("Hey, Bali," she said teasingly. "Talk to me! I want to find someone to talk to!") The bird raised a round eye at her, then laughed loudly. Yeadan also burst out laughing miserably and gave a biscuit to the parrot. After that, they became good friends.

Mic saw that she was eloquent but not naive, and over time, he inevitably fell for her natural beauty. When Niis was in a bad mood, he would even protect her.

She arranged everything in the house in an orderly manner. At night, when she played cards with Mic, she couldn't help but often glance at the unmoved Niis, but she didn't go any further to stir up his emotions. She happily handled daily chores. One day, Niis told her impetuously that she could take (Mrs. Parrot), her eyes melted immediately, she didn't know how to thank him, but he walked out of the house without saying a word.

("You know, Mic, I don't want to go back," Yeadan said suddenly enthusiastically. ("No,") she said softly. ("These four weeks have been like (planting fragrance! Exotic fragrance.)

Yeadan saw Niis at the dock. He had come to welcome the new engineers. She shouted to him.

("Are you here to say goodbye to me?") she asked eagerly.

He turned and walked up to her, smiling at her slightly for the first time.

("Goodbye, kid,") he patted her arm. (Unfinished)
